linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mrow><msub><mrow><mi>I</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>SS</mi></mrow></msub><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><mi>V</mi><mo>−</mo><msub><mrow><mi>I</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Ω</mi><mo>,</mo><mn>0</mn></mrow></msub><msub><mrow><mi>R</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>i</mi><mo>,</mo><mn>0</mn></mrow></msub><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mrow><msub><mrow><mi>I</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Ω</mi><mo>,</mo><mn>0</mn></mrow></msub><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><mi>V</mi><mo>−</mo><msub><mrow><mi>I</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>SS</mi></mrow></msub><msub><mrow><mi>R</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>i</mi><mo>,</mo><mi>SS</mi></mrow></msub><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow></mfrac></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p last">Δ<i>V</i> represents the polarization potential, and <i>I</i><sub>Ω,0</sub> and <i>I</i><sub><i>ss</i></sub> denote the currents at pristine and steady states, respectively. <i>R</i><sub><i>i</i>,0</sub> and <i>R</i><sub><i>i</i>,<i>ss</i></sub> represent the interfacial resistances of the Na∥Na symmetric batteries before and after CA measurement, respectively.</div></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Theoretical Calculations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were performed by using the projector augmented plane-wave method within the Vienna ab initio simulation package (VASP). The generalized gradient approximation (GGA) was used in the Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof scheme  (PBE) to describe the exchange-correlation function. The cutoff energy for the plane wave was set to 450 eV. The energy criterion was set to 10<sup>–4</sup> eV in the iterative solution of the Kohn–Sham equation. All of the structures were relaxed until the residual forces on the atoms declined to less than 0.05 eV/Å. A Monkhorst–Pack scheme with a k-point mesh of 1 × 1 × 1 was used. The diffusion barrier of adsorbed hydrogen at different adsorption sites was explored by using the nudge elastic band (CI-NEB) method. MD simulation was performed using Materials Studio software 18.1. For pure electrolyte system, 130 PC, 130 EC, 30 Na<sup>+</sup>, and 30 FSI<sup>–</sup> were added to the 3.78 × 3.78 × 3.78 nm<sup>3</sup> simulation box. The original amorphous structure was built after the model was relaxed using Forcite with a COMPASS force field. The molecular dynamics (MD) simulation was then carried out using the COMPASS force field. In the MD simulations, the starting amorphous structure was formed at a lower density of 1.00 g cm<sup>–3</sup>. Furthermore, this system was relaxed by an NPT procedure for 1000 ps, all with an Andersen thermostat, to ensure that the system was in equilibrium. For the analysis of the structural and diffusion characteristics, a 1000 ps NPT simulation was used. The time step in the simulations mentioned above was 1 fs.</div></div><div id="sec2_6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> COMSOL Simulation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">COMSOL Multiphysics 5.6 with the implemented finite element solver was used to study the flux distributions in the bare Na and the ZMOF-NSC@Na anode during the Na plating process. The Nernst–Planck–Poisson equation was employed to depict the deposition behavior of Na<sup>+</sup>, and the Butler–Volmer kinetic equation was used to introduce the reaction on the anode surface. The initial Na<sup>+</sup> concentration and current density of Na plating were set to 1 mol L<sup>–1</sup> and 3.3 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>, respectively. The length of the anode was 50 μm before simulation, and the distance between them was 50 μm. The protuberance on the Na anode surface was represented by a semiellipse with a semimajor axis of 4 μm, and the thickness of the added material was set to 5 μm.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">To address the challenges of the growth of Na dendrites and safety concerns, we employed a straightforward fabrication approach, creating a multifunctional artificial interphase layer by directly rolling selected MOF-808 powder onto the surface of Na metal, capitalizing on its large cavity size and high chemical stability, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a. The resultant coating is nearly transparent upon visual examination and is suitable for roll-to-roll production, which aligns well with industrial battery manufacturing processes. More importantly, our method of incorporating MOFs into sodium anodes proves to be universally applicable, opening avenues for selecting or modulating various MOFs for SMBs, as demonstrated by the feasibility validated in this study. To investigate the accuracy of the designed ZMOF-NSC structure, a series of characterizations were performed on the pure MOF and modified Na anode. The phase structure of as-prepared MOF powder was confirmed by the XRD pattern of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b, and it is evident that the diffraction peaks observed at 8.39, 10.14, and 11.02° are respectively attributed to the (311), (400), and (331) crystallographic planes of MOF-808, exhibiting precise correspondence to a previous report. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> FTIR confirmed that the phase structure of the MOF powder, delineated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c, reveals a pronounced and broad absorption peak at 3390 cm<sup>–1</sup>, attributable to the O–H vibrational modes. Typically, the absorption bands at 1560 and 1387 cm<sup>–1</sup> are ascribed to the asymmetric and symmetric stretching vibrations of the COO<sup>–</sup> groups within the benzene-tricarboxylate moiety, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> The bands at 657 cm<sup>–1</sup> are indicative of the stretching modes of Zr–O clusters, which  further elucidated the structure of ZMOF-NSC@Na. Furthermore, the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms of the MOF, depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d, manifest a quintessential type-I isotherm, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> yielding a surface area of 531.704 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> with a pore volume of 0.59 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>. Additionally, the microporous structure of the MOF, featuring a pore size of 0.785 nm, confines FSI<sup>–</sup> anions and thereby limits their mobility, and the smaller Na<sup>+</sup> ions experience enhanced mobility through the MOF lattice, ultimately increasing the transfer number. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was employed to detect the ingredients and bondings in the synthesized MOF and ZMOF-NSC@Na. The analysis of the high-resolution Zr 3d spectrum in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e presented two distinct peaks, located at 182.4 and 186.8 eV, that are identified as Zr–O and Zr–Zr, respectively, indicative of the Zr<sup>4+</sup> within the MOF framework. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> Concurrently, the O 1s spectrum illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>f reveals three oxygen states associated with O–C (531.8 eV), O═C (532.9 eV), and O–Zr (530.1 eV), which are in alignment with the individual characterization results of the MOF nanoparticles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1b</a>). Furthermore, the F 1s spectrum, displaying a sole peak at 687.8 eV in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2b</a>, is attributed to the F–C bond from PVDF. Hence, these findings illustrate the successful fabrication and synthesis of ZMOF-NSC on Na metal, demonstrating its potential for practical SMAs with a simple, cost-effective, and scalable approach as well as its adequate mechanical strength to impede the growth of dendrites and safeguard the interface layer against penetration by sodium dendrites. In addition, the corresponding energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) showed the regular octahedral morphology of MOF grains with a uniform distribution of Zr, C, and O elements (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>), while scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images displayed a flat and dense surface on ZMOF-NSC@Na (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). Also, EDS mapping verified the uniform distribution of these elements across the surface of the electrode, and cross-sectional views showcase a dense MOF layer with a thickness of 103.5 μm on ZMOF-NSC@Na (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>).</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/medium/am4c09387_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/large/am4c09387_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Schematic illustration of the preparation process of the ZMOF-NSC@Na anode. (b) XRD patterns of MOF and ZMOF-NSC@Na. (c) FT-IR spectra of ZMOF-NSC@Na. (d) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms of MOF, with the inset showing the micropore size distribution. (e) Zr 3d, (f) O 1s, and (g) C 1s spectra of ZMOF-NSC@Na.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/large/am4c09387_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09387&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/medium/am4c09387_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/large/am4c09387_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) SEM images of the surface and cross-section of ZMOF-NSC@Na and corresponding elemental mapping results. (b) Tafel plots of bare Na and ZMOF-NSC@Na anodes. (c) Calculated activation energies of the batteries. (d) The Na<sup>+</sup> of ZMOF-NSC@Na symmetric batteries and inset showing the Nyquist plots of impedance before and after polarization.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/large/am4c09387_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09387&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further elucidate the significant role of the ZMOF-NSC layer as a robust primary SEI in promoting rapid Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and achieving dendrite-free SMBs, the electrochemical dynamics of the artificial ZMOF-NSC interlayers were investigated. First, the electrolyte wettability of ZMOF-NSC@Na and bare Na was quantitatively evaluated through the determination of contact angles, as illustrated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>. The analysis revealed a contact angle of 47.1° for the electrolyte on the bare Na, indicating moderate wettability. In contrast, the electrolyte displayed a decreased contact angle (29.8°) when in contact with the ZMOF nanosheet coating on Na, evidencing a significantly enhanced electrolyte affinity. Besides, due to the robust adhesion of the ZMOF layer on the Na metal surface, ZMOF@Na can effectively mitigate side reactions with electrolyte despite the high reactivity of Na metal. In an experimental setup where bare Na and ZMOF@Na were exposed to the electrolyte for 3 days (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>), the surface of bare Na exhibited a dark-black discoloration and edge curling due to reactions with the electrolyte that produced byproducts. In contrast, ZMOF@Na maintained partial original adherence to the Na metal, demonstrating that the ZMOF layer provides effective protection for sodium metal and exhibits superior stability against the electrolyte. Exchange current density (I<sub>0</sub>) measurements from symmetric batteries constructed with both bare Na and ZMOF-NSC@Na elucidated the ion transport efficiency across the dual-layered SEI. As described in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b, the I<sub>0</sub> for the ZMOF-NSC interface was measured at 1.0619 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>, significantly surpassing those observed for the bare Na (0.6388 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>), which highlights the superior charge transfer proficiency afforded by the synthetic ZMOF-NSC protective layer. To further explain the ZMOF-NSC protective layer’s impact on ion transport mechanisms, the activation energy (E<sub><i>a</i></sub>) was determined by fitting temperature-varying Nyquist data (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8a,b</a>) to the Arrhenius model (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> The activation energy for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion through the SEI of ZMOF-NSC is 74.16 kJ mol<sup>–1</sup>, compared to 78.30 kJ mol<sup>–1</sup> for bare Na, which proves that Na<sup>+</sup> can pass through the modified artificial layer more easily and diminishes the polarization within the battery throughout the cycling process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 27 and 28">(27,28)</a> Additionally, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>, the Na<sup>+</sup> transference number (<i>t</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>) for ZMOF-NSC is 0.93, surpassing that of the bare Na metal anode (0.80). The variation in <i>t</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub> can be attributed to the difference in the penetration ability within the interface layer.</div><div class="NLM_p">Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were conducted to explore the molecular interactions involved in constructing the Na<sup>+</sup> migration pathway and to elucidate the mechanisms behind the designed ZMOF-NSC. As depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a, the ZMOF structure, characterized by its abundant adsorption sites and an orderly pore configuration, establishes a robust basis for Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption. After the MOF structure was simplified and correlated with the experimental results, four distinct adsorption sites were identified and analyzed. Notably, the adsorption energy at vacancies between two ligand benzene rings was the highest among the sites considered, indicating site 2 as the most favorable for Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption. Additionally, sites near Zr<sup>4+</sup> exhibited lower adsorption energy but have a higher binding energy with FSI<sup>–</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). Hence, it can be deduced that the ZMOF, with its extensive metal cluster framework, serves a dual function: it not only facilitates Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption, thereby enhancing diffusion, but also promotes partial desolvation. DFT simulations modeling the deposition of varying numbers of Na atoms on ZMOF-NSC elucidated the reasons for uniform Na<sup>+</sup> deposition, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b. The deposition process was specifically analyzed for three Na atoms, during which the Gibbs free energy consistently remained negative, indicating the good adsorption of Na atoms at the interface. Importantly, the diffusion barrier of Na<sup>+</sup> within the artificial SEI layer constitutes a significant factor to consider at the anode interface, as the migration of Na<sup>+</sup> can reflect the electrochemical reactions occurring within the battery. In an effort to delineate the diffusion pathways of Na<sup>+</sup> within ZMOF-NSC with a higher degree of specificity, we posit that four paths may broadly be classified into two categories: one mechanism involves Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion along the metallic scaffolding, and another hypothesis suggests Na<sup>+</sup> migration through the framework’s cavities. The hypothesis was supported by simulations of four potential pathways, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>. Significantly elevated diffusion barriers characterize paths 3 and 4, whereas paths 1 and 2 exhibited relatively lower barriers, suggesting a more facile and unhindered migration of Na<sup>+</sup> through the latter pathways. The detailed migration paths are graphically delineated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S11–S13</a>. These observations affirm the sodium affinity of ZMOF-NSC, emphasize the rapid migration of Na<sup>+</sup> within ZMOF-NSC, and demonstrate its efficacy in inhibiting dendrite formation, thereby revealing the material’s potential to enhance the performance of the battery.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/medium/am4c09387_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/large/am4c09387_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) The corresponding adsorption energy for four adsorption sites. (b) DFT simulation of the adsorption of different numbers of Na atoms on the most stable sites. (c) Contrast diagram of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barriers.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/large/am4c09387_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09387&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical stability has been preliminarily justified in the long-term Na plating/stripping, demonstrating superior cycling stability exceeding 2500 h as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a. The voltage profile of the ZMOF-NSC@Na symmetric battery (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>) remained consistently flat and smooth throughout  cycling, exhibiting a modest voltage polarization of 50 mV. This performance demonstrates the effectiveness of the engineered protective dual-layered SEI in ensuring rapid Na<sup>+</sup> transport. In contrast, the bare Na symmetric battery manifested a considerably shorter lifespan of 150 h and encountered a high voltage polarization (200 mV), which suggests the degradation of Na<sup>+</sup> under the conventional natural SEI layer, likely resulting from the growth of dendrites and leading to short-circuiting. Optical photographs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16</a>) and SEM images after cycling (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a>) revealed that the bare Na surface exhibits significant degradation with irregularities and extensive dendrite formation, leading to substantial electrolyte consumption and sluggish diffusion kinetics that severely impair the performance. In contrast, ZMOF-NSC maintained a robust bond with the sodium metal and exhibited only slight surface damage, demonstrating its durability. Additionally, the ZMOF-NSC@Na batteries also exhibited prolonged cycle durations of 800  and 500 h at elevated current densities of 0.5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>/0.5 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b) and 1 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>/1 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a>), respectively. Correspondingly, the highly reversible and stable Na plating/stripping process with the protection of dual-layered SEI is among the excellent SMBs when compared to those previously reported (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). These results substantiate the enhanced stability of the Na anode, attributable to the beneficial effects of ZMOF-NSC-induced dual-layered SEI. Additionally, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d shows the rate behaviors of the ZMOF-NSC@Na and bare Na batteries at a current density ranging from 0.5 to 5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> with a fixed capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>. Notably, the ZMOF-NSC@Na symmetric battery demonstrated significantly reduced voltage polarization across all tested current densities, with the disparity amplified alongside the current increase. Conversely, the bare Na symmetric battery exhibited a voltage instability at 5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>. The observed findings indicate that the engineered ZMOF-NSC-derived dual-layered SEI, particularly when subjected to elevated current densities, is capable of facilitating uniform ion flux across the heterogeneous interface, thereby ensuring  the homogeneity of Na<sup>+</sup> deposition. The superiority of ZMOF-NSC was further confirmed by the measurement of the Aurbach coulombic efficiency (CE) of Na∥Cu half batteries (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e), which demonstrated a higher CE of 96.65% for ZMOF-NSC@Na compared to that of bare Na (92.51%). At a current density of 0.5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>, the voltage hysteresis (115 mV) of ZMOF-NSC@Na, which was smaller than that of the bare Na (200 mV), as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19</a>, demonstrates that ZMOF facilitates the formation of SEI with the fast migration of Na<sup>+</sup>.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/medium/am4c09387_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/large/am4c09387_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Cycling performance of ZMOF-NSC@Na and bare Na symmetric batteries at 0.25 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>/0.25 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>. (b) Cycling performance of ZMOF-NSC@Na and bare Na symmetric batteries at 0.5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>/0.5 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> (c) Comparison of the cycling stability of previously reported Na anodes. (d) Rate performance comparison of ZMOF-NSC@Na and bare Na symmetric batteries with a fixed capacity of 1 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup> at current densities of 0.5, 1, 2, 3, 4, and 5 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>, respectively. (e) Voltage curves of the Na∥Cu half batteries in accurate CE tests.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/large/am4c09387_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09387&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Compared to bare Na, ZMOF-NSC@Na exhibited superior performance in terms of both interfacial and charge transfer impedance before and after cycling (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a,b). The introduction of ZMOF-NSC reduces interface impedance, indicating the formation of a high Na<sup>+</sup> transfer interfacial film, while the lower charge transfer impedance suggests a quicker desolvation process of Na<sup>+</sup> in the carbonate electrolyte, contrasting with the higher charge transfer impedance that leads to the poor cycling performance of bare Na batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Besides, finite element analysis conducted through COMSOL Multiphysics elucidated the current density distribution on the surfaces of ZMOF-NSC@Na and bare Na anodes. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d reveals a nonuniform current distribution on the bare Na, emphasizing a significant “tip effect” on its crystalline surface, as indicated by red dots. The uneven deposition of Na layers likely resulted in the erratic growth of dendrites, increasing the risk of the separator being punctured and the potential for short-circuiting. In contrast, with the aid of ZMOF-NSC-derived dual-layered SEI, the uniformity of current distribution on the Na anode surface was markedly improved, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c, effectively reducing risks.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/medium/am4c09387_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/large/am4c09387_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Nyquist plots of (a) ZMOF-NSC@Na and (b) bare Na symmetric batteries before and after the cycle. COMSOL simulation of the local current density distribution for (c) ZMOF-NSC@Na and (d) the bare Na anode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/large/am4c09387_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09387&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further demonstrate the Na<sup>+</sup> deposition regulated by the ZMOF-NSC-derived dual-layered SEI, the SEM images were used to highlight the regulated Na surface after cycling. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b displays that the bare Na anode without the protection of ZMOF-NSC shows a rough surface morphology consisting of numerous sharp protrusions and cracks, indicating an uneven deposition process during Na<sup>+</sup> plating and a persistent issue of uncontrolled dendrite aggregation alongside the accumulation of byproducts, which likely leads to separator penetration and subsequent short circuits. Conversely, with the protection of ZMOF-NSC-derived dual-layered SEI, the Na anode exhibited smoother, denser surfaces and the absence of parasitic reaction products (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S20</a>). It is evident that homogeneous Na deposition is achieved through uniform nucleation sites and consistent Na<sup>+</sup> flux, facilitated by the designed ZMOF-NSC interfaces, which effectively inhibit dendrite formation. In the quest to elucidate the prolonged cycling stability of ZMOF-NSC@Na, the battery was disassembled for further examination after 500 cycles. Surprisingly, the anode interface unveiled the presence of spherical sodium deposition, an unexpected morphology highlighted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c,d. The formation of spherical sodium deposition under SEI is driven by diffusion-reaction dynamics. Under diffusion-controlled conditions, a low Na<sup>+</sup> concentration beneath the SEI promotes dendrite growth. Enhancing Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion within the SEI shifts deposition dynamics to reaction-controlled, increasing Na ion availability at nucleation sites, leading to uniform spherical deposition with smooth surfaces. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> Furthermore, among all morphologies, spherical sodium exhibits a minimal surface area per unit volume, leading to a uniform formation of the SEI between fresh sodium and the electrolyte, improving CE and the cycling stability of SMBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> When maintaining the same electrode reaction rate, the introduction of a ZMOF-NSC-derived dual-layered SEI effectively alters the kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion from slow to fast. This change is evidenced by the presence of spherical Na deposition after 500 h of cycling, which significantly increases the ratio of sphere/all deposition compared to the bare Na. The enhanced Na ion concentration beneath the SEI contributes to the modulation of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and reaction kinetics, aiding in the suppression of the growth of dendrites and thereby enhancing the safety and longevity of SMBs. In situ optical microscopy observations further corroborate these deposition behaviors. As depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>e, bare Na exhibited distinct protrusions that appear on the surface within 20 min of deposition. Such formations lead to markedly uneven sodium deposition and subsequently develop into mossy dendrites, culminating in short circuits and pronounced performance degradation. In contrast, ZMOF-NSC@Na consistently manifested smooth and dense structural integrity and compact morphology with homogeneous Na<sup>+</sup> deposition during the whole electrochemical plating process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>f). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> These in situ observations unequivocally demonstrated that the ZMOF-NSC-derived dual-layered SEI is effective at mitigating Na dendrite formation. Overall, these findings conclusively demonstrate the significantly beneficial effect of the engineered dual-layered SEI in the uniform deposition of Na ions and achieving faster kinetics, which is expected to enhance the cycling stability of SMBs.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/medium/am4c09387_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/large/am4c09387_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) SEM images of ZMOF-NSC@Na and (b) the bare Na anode after cycling for 30 h at 1 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>/1 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>. (c and d) SEM images of the ZMOF-NSC@Na anode after cycling for 500 h at 1 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>/1 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>. In situ optical microscopy observation of the Na plating process for (e) bare Na and (f) ZMOF-NSC@Na at 1.0 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/large/am4c09387_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09387&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To better elucidate the composition of the dual-layered SEI induced by ZMOF-NSC, we conducted in-depth XPS analysis. The elemental distribution after Ar<sup>+</sup> etching, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a and b, highlights the differences in the atomic ratios of C 1s, O 1, and F 1s within the induced SEI layers. Notably, the reduced intensity of the C–O (285.6 eV) and C–C (284.8 eV) peaks, along with the absence of signatures for C═O (288.1 eV) and C═C (283.0 eV) after etching on ZMOF-NSC@Na, indicates that ZMOF-NSC induced the second SEI interface, significantly suppressing the decomposition of EC and PC during Na plating/stripping. Additionally, while a substantial O content of 53% is observed on the bare Na anode after Ar<sup>+</sup> etching (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S21a</a>), ZMOF-NSC@Na shows a significantly reduced oxygen content of 24% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S21b</a>). This reduction in the oxygen presence indicates that ZMOF-NSC@Na has a thinner SEI layer enriched with inorganics compared to the bare Na. Moreover, the presence of the organic compound ROCO<sub>2</sub>Na (289.5 eV) in the outer layer of the SEI from ZMOF-NSC@Na stands in contrast to the SEI on bare Na anodes, where organic byproducts exist in both the inner and outer layers. The O 1s spectra reinforce the significant presence of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (532.2 eV) and Na<sub>2</sub>O (530.8 eV) in the ZMOF-NSC-induced SEI layer, compounds noted for their high Young’s modulus and mechanical strength, which effectively suppress the growth of dendrites and enhance interface integrity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a> The inorganic components showed a gradient distribution within the SEI layer, accompanied by a marked reduction in the ROCO<sub>2</sub>Na (533.8 eV) peak intensity as the etching depth increases. The F 1s spectral analysis further demonstrates a more homogeneous SEI composition on ZMOF-NSC@Na relative to bare Na, with progressive intensification of the NaF (684.2 eV) peak in deeper layers. This homogeneity is corroborated by the elevated F content in ZMOF-NSC@Na compared to bare Na, indicating a higher prevalence of NaF in the former. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> It is suggest that the introduction of ZMOF-NSC leads to the formation of an SEI layer with an organic-rich exterior and an inorganic-dense interior as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S22</a>.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/medium/am4c09387_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/large/am4c09387_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. XPS depth profile for C 1s, O 1s, and F 1s spectra of (a) ZMOF-NSC@Na and (b) the bare Na anode after cycling for 30 h at 1 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>/1 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>. Schematic diagrams of (c) the mechanism for the growth of sodium dendrites at the bare Na interface. (d) The mechanism for the spherical deposition by constructing the ZMOF-NSC interface.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/large/am4c09387_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09387&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To confirm the enduring effectiveness of the SEI induced by ZMOF-NSC, XPS analyses were performed after 500 h of cycling under conditions of 1 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>/1 mAh cm<sup>–2</sup>. Despite extended cycling, the SEI remained robust, characterized by a rich composition of inorganic constituents (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S23</a>). Specifically, in the N 1s spectra (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S23d</a>), the detection of organic N–S bonds at 399.8 eV and inorganic Na<sub>3</sub>N at 398.8 eV indicates effective decomposition of FSI<sup>–</sup> by ZMOF-NSC. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> In the S 2p spectra (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S23e</a>), the presence of RSO<sub>2</sub>F (170.0/171.1 eV), N–S (168.9 eV), and SO<sub arrange="stagger">3</sub><sup arrange="stagger">2–</sup> (166.8 eV), along with the subsequent emergence of Na<sub>2</sub>S (161.4 eV) and C–S (163.4 eV) after etching, demonstrates a compositional shift toward Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>S accumulation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 37 and 38">(37,38)</a> The F 1s spectra (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S23c</a>) show a decrease in organic S–F (688.2 eV) signals, while NaF (683.6 eV) remains constant, further emphasizing the inorganics in the deeper layers of the SEI. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> This detailed analysis highlights a clear compositional stratification within the SEI: an organic-rich outer layer and an inorganic-rich inner layer. Molecular dynamics (MD) simulations were conducted to elucidate the solvated sheath of Na. The radial distribution functions (RDF) and coordination numbers (N(r)), illustrated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S24a</a>, demonstrate an enhanced intensity of the Na–O<sub>FSI<sup>–</sup></sub> peak at a distance of 2.3 Å due to the introduction of ZMOF-NSC in contrast to the relatively lower coordination tendency with bare Na (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S24b</a>). This observation suggests that the majority of FSI<sup>–</sup> enters the solvated sheath of Na<sup>+</sup>, facilitating the formation of an SEI layer enriched with inorganic components. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref40 ref41 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref40 ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 36 and 40 to 42">(36,40−42)</a> To elucidate the suppression mechanism of Na dendrite formation by the ZMOF-NSC interfacial layer, scenarios depicting potential Na deposition and dendrite formation at various interfaces were analyzed, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>c. The unmodified interface leads to an uneven electric field distribution and direct contact between the anode and electrolyte, which promotes epitaxial nucleation and the growth of Na dendrites. Conversely, the robust protective layer of ZMOF-NSC (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>d), tightly bound to the sodium metal, provides abundant sodium-affinitive sites, facilitating Na<sup>+</sup> transport through the MOF’s intricate 3D channels, thereby inducing spherical deposition and significantly inhibiting dendrite formation.</div><div class="NLM_p">Further assessments of the electrolyte compatibility under real operation scenarios and electrochemical performances of full batteries were conducted. Remarkably, NVP∥ZMOF-NSC@Na batteries exhibited a sustained specific capacity of 79.91 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 2000 cycles at 10 C, with an exemplary average CE of 99.86% and minimized polarization, as detailed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>c, which demonstrates that ZMOF-NSC exhibits excellent protection of the anode. Nevertheless, under the same conditions, the NVP∥bare Na batteries observed a rapidly increasing charge/discharge polarization (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>a,d), showing extremely poor cycling stability due to the severe side reactions and dendrite formation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> At an elevated current density of 20 C, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>b, NVP∥ZMOF-NSC@Na batteries astonishingly demonstrated a stable cycling performance over 3000 cycles, in contrast to the rapid capacity fade observed in bare Na, which can be attributed to the fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport improved by ZMOF-NSC. Compared with recent works on full batteries, this method evidently outperforms other earlier-developed research, as demonstrated by the superior overall performance, particularly in terms of high-capacity retention and cycle life (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>e shows the rate performance of the NVP∥ZMOF-NSC@Na battery. As the current density increased, the NVP∥bare Na battery showed a much faster capacity drop in comparison to that of the NVP∥ZMOF-NSC@Na battery. When the current density was set back to 0.1 C, the NVP∥ZMOF-NSC@Na battery still showed a specific capacity of 111.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and retained stable cycling. In addition, similar and pronounced flat voltage plateaus were presented in the charge/discharge profiles at different current rates (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S25</a>), suggesting fast reaction kinetics. Furthermore, even under a more harsh capacity (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S26</a>), the NVP∥ZMOF-NSC@Na batteries still deliver high capacities, particularly at 50 C. However, when the capacity of the bare Na decayed to 0 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, NVP∥ZMOF-NSC@Na batteries still maintained a high capacity of 71.08 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Compared with recent works on full batteries, this method significantly outperforms earlier developments, showing superior overall performance, especially in high-capacity retention and cycle life (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>). Additionally, NVP∥ZMOF-NSC@Na batteries demonstrate a remarkable energy density of 370 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> and a higher power density of 10484 W kg<sup>–1</sup> (based on the mass of NVP), outperforming previous research on sodium-ion full cells as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>b. Despite the energy density not attaining the apex levels previously documented when solely evaluated based on the cathode mass, the incorporation of the MOFs characterized by a low atomic mass offers a considerable benefit in reducing the overall mass of batteries. This novel approach, which employs an artificial dual-layered SEI protection strategy, demonstrates considerable promise for enhancing the performance of high-energy-density metal batteries and offers significant compatibility advantages, enabling its extension to other conventional cathode materials to achieve excellent cycle performance.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/medium/am4c09387_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/large/am4c09387_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Cycling performance and the CE curves of NVP∥ZMOF-NSC@Na and NVP∥bare Na batteries at (a) 10 and (b) 20 C. Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles for (c) NVP∥ZMOF-NSC@Na and (d) NVP∥bare Na batteries obtained at a 10 C rate after cycling. (e) Rate performance of NVP∥ZMOF-NSC@Na and NVP∥bare Na batteries with different Na plating capacities.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/large/am4c09387_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09387&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig9" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/medium/am4c09387_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/large/am4c09387_0009.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. (a) Comparison of the electrochemical performances with the previously reported sodium-ion full batteries. (b)  Ragone plots showing energy and power densities of NVP∥ZMOF-NSC@Na batteries versus other references.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/asset/images/large/am4c09387_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09387&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i13">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">We have developed  ZMOF-NSC, a novel and effective artificial SEI that enhances rapid ion transfer kinetics and provides outstanding durability, successfully preventing dendrite formation. The meticulously designed optimal ionic channels and highly ordered pore structures of ZMOF-NSC are crucial for promoting a uniform Na ion flux and enhancing the protective measures for the Na anode. Comprehensive experimental and theoretical studies confirm that the strategic incorporation of ZMOF-NSC provides the channels for Na<sup>+</sup> migration and ensures Na ion flux through the interface layer, effectively facilitating the partial desolvation of Na<sup>+</sup> and fostering the in situ formation of a robust, dual-layered SEI enriched with inorganics (NaF, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>O, etc.) possessing a high Young’s modulus and mechanical strength to effectively suppress the growth of dendrites and enhance interface integrity. Symmetric cells equipped with ZMOF-NSC demonstrate ultrastable performance, with the batteries exhibiting a remarkable lifespan exceeding 2500 h and homogeneous Na<sup>+</sup> deposition. The superior performance of the NVP∥ZMOF-NSC@Na batteries, which retained 81% of their capacity over 2000 cycles at 10 C and continued stable operation through 3000 cycles at 20 C, underscores the practical efficacy of ZMOF-NSC in practical applications. Moreover, NVP∥ZMOF-NSC@Na batteries exhibit an impressive energy density of 370 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> and a high power density of 10484 W kg<sup>–1</sup>. This work underscores the pivotal role of MOFs in enhancing the structural and chemical integrity of SEI layers, crucial for mitigating issues such as uneven ion distribution and the growth of dendrites. Additionally, the meticulous engineering of self-assembled pore structures and surface active sites within MOFs not only offers a scalable pathway to establish foundational control over MOFs within SMBs but also shows considerable promise for promoting the development of long-lasting, dendrite-free electrode structures in metal-based rechargeable batteries.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i14">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c09387?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c09387</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Figures showing an electrochemical performance (rate and long-term cycling) comparison of different current densities; voltage profiles; Raman spectra; MD simulations; COMSOL simulations; SEM images; XPS spectra, EIS plots; Arrhenius behavior; and supporting tables (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09387/suppl_file/am4c09387_si_001.pdf">am4c09387_si_001.pdf (2.56 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.4c09387" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Bridging multiscale interfaces for developing ionically conductive high-voltage iron sulfate-containing sodium-based battery positive electrodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Jiyu; Yan, Yongliang; Wang, Xin; Cui, Yanyan; Zhang, Zhengfeng; Wang, Sen; Xie, Zhengkun; Yan, Pengfei; Chen, Weihua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2023</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3701</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NCAOBW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2041-1723</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Portfolio</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Non-aq. sodium-ion batteries (SiBs) are a viable electrochem. energy storage system for grid storage.  However, the practical development of SiBs is hindered mainly by the sluggish kinetics and interfacial instability of pos.-electrode active materials, such as polyanion-type iron-based sulfates, at high voltage.  Here, to circumvent these issues, we proposed the multiscale interface engineering of Na2.26Fe1.87(SO4)3, where bulk heterostructure and exposed crystal plane were tuned to improve the Na-ion storage performance.  Physicochem. characterizations and theor. calcns. suggested that the heterostructure of Na6Fe(SO4)4 phase facilitated ionic kinetics by densifying Na-ion migration channels and lowering energy barriers.  The (11-2) plane of Na2.26Fe1.87(SO4)3 promoted the adsorption of the electrolyte soln. ClO4- anions and fluoroethylene carbonate mols., which formed an inorg.-rich Na-ion conductive interphase at the pos. electrode.  When tested in combination with a presodiated FeS/carbon-based neg. electrode in lab.- scale single-layer pouch cell configuration, the Na2.26Fe1.87(SO4)3-based pos. electrode enables an initial discharge capacity of about 83.9 mAh g-1, an av. cell discharge voltage of 2.35 V and a specific capacity retention of around 97% after 40 cycles at 24 mA g-1 and 25 °C.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoFw0E10moBv7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lixjH_rzggcPw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3sXhtlalu7rP&amp;md5=1bc6277360c403679938ad4f06479bcf</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, C.-C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, Y.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qin, J.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Q.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A honeycomb-like bulk superstructure of carbon nanosheets for electrocatalysis and energy storage</span>. <i>Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>59</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">19627</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">19632</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/anie.202004737</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D59%26publication_year%3D2020%26pages%3D19627-19632%26journal%3DAngew.%2BChem.%252C%2BInt.%2BEd.%26author%3DL.%2BZou%26author%3DC.-C.%2BHou%26author%3DQ.%2BWang%26author%3DY.-S.%2BWei%26author%3DZ.%2BLiu%26author%3DJ.-S.%2BQin%26author%3DH.%2BPang%26author%3DQ.%2BXu%26title%3DA%2Bhoneycomb-like%2Bbulk%2Bsuperstructure%2Bof%2Bcarbon%2Bnanosheets%2Bfor%2Belectrocatalysis%2Band%2Benergy%2Bstorage%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fanie.202004737&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c09387&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fanie.202004737&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Honeycomb-Like Bulk Superstructure of Carbon Nanosheets for Electrocatalysis and Energy Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zou, Lianli; Hou, Chun-Chao; Wang, Qiuju; Wei, Yong-Sheng; Liu, Zheng; Qin, Jun-Sheng; Pang, Huan; Xu, Qiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">59</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">44</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">19627-19632</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACIEF5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1433-7851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Superstructures have attracted extensive attention because of their potential applications in materials science and biol.  Herein, the first centimeter-sized porous superstructure of carbon nanosheets (SCNS) is fabricated by using metal-org. framework nanoparticles as a template and polyvinylpyrrolidone as an addnl. carbon source.  The SCNS shows a honeycomb-like morphol. with wall-sharing carbon cages, in each cavity of which a porous carbon sphere is encapsulated.  A single piece of SCNS is directly used as the electrode for a two-electrode sym. supercapacitor cell without any binders and supports, benefiting from its advantage in ultra-large geometric size, and the Fe-immobilized SCNS exhibits excellent catalytic performances for oxygen redn. reaction and in a Zn-air battery.  This synthetic strategy presents a facile approach for prepg. functional SCNS at centimetric scale with controllable morphologies and compns. favoring the fabrication of energy devices.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoTj57EZlg-VrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgLBFA4U5D_Ng"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhtVaiurjE&amp;md5=e6da7a07f4dff9958955a335170a2aa3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1016/j.jcis.2024.04.044"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zeng, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fan, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Micro-mesoporous cobalt phosphosulfide (Co3S4/CoP/NC) nanowires for ultrahigh rate capacity and ultrastable sodium ion battery</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Heterogeneous NASICON-Type Composite as Low-Cost, High-Performance Cathode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Guo, Jin-Zhi; Zhang, Hong-Xia; Gu, Zhen-Yi; Du, Miao; Lue, Hong-Yan; Zhao, Xin-Xin; Yang, Jia-Lin; Li, Wen-Hao; Kang, Shuwen; Zou, Weimin; Wu, Xing-Long</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">32</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2209482</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">As promising cathode for sodium-ion batteries, Na+ Superionic Conductor (NASICON)-type materials have attracted attention owing to their excellent structural stability, superior ionic cond., and small vol. expansion.  However, the vanadium-based NASICON-type cathode with the biotoxicity and exorbitant price of V element and the iron-based cathode with low mean working voltage as well as the intrinsic poor electronic cond. of polyanionic compds. hinder their practical applications.  Herein, a double-carbon-layer decorated heterogeneous composite, Na3V2(PO4)3-Na3Fe2(PO4)(P2O7) (NVFPP/C/G), is successfully prepd. for addressing these limitations.  Due to their synergistic effect, NVFPP/C/G exhibits excellent electrochem. performance in half-cell system and superior full-cell performance when matched with hard carbon anode.  Furthermore, the phase compn., electrode kinetics, and phase transition are confirmed by combined analyses of slow scanning power X-ray diffraction, high-resoln. transmission electron microscopy, cyclic voltammetry with various scan rates, galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique, ex situ X-ray photoelectron spectra, and in situ X-ray diffraction.  This study portends a promising strategy to utilize composite structure engineering for developing advanced polyanionic cathodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpJOJOyxolG4bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgLBFA4U5D_Ng"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB38Xis1yqt7bM&amp;md5=9c1ee6eda8d5a0f8a75a548932a78829</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemrev.8b00642"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Paek, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mitlin, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, S. W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium metal anodes: emerging solutions to dendrite growth</span>. <i>Chem.                 10.1021/acs.chemrev.8b00642</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D119%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D5416-5460%26journal%3DChem.%2BRev.%26author%3DB.%2BLee%26author%3DE.%2BPaek%26author%3DD.%2BMitlin%26author%3DS.%2BW.%2BLee%26title%3DSodium%2Bmetal%2Banodes%253A%2Bemerging%2Bsolutions%2Bto%2Bdendrite%2Bgrowth%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facs.chemrev.8b00642&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c09387&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facs.chemrev.8b00642&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium metal anodes: Emerging solutions to dendrite growth</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lee, Byeongyong; Paek, Eunsu; Mitlin, David; Lee, Seung Woo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Reviews (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">119</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5416-5460</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CHREAY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0009-2665</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">This comprehensive Review focuses on the key challenges and recent progress regarding sodium-metal anodes employed in sodium-metal batteries (SMBs).  The metal anode is the essential component of emerging energy storage systems such as sodium sulfur and sodium selenium, which are discussed as example full-cell applications.  We begin with a description of the differences in the chem. and phys. properties of Na metal vs. the oft-studied Li metal, and a corresponding discussion regarding the no. of ways in which Na does not follow Li-inherited paradigms in its electrochem. behavior.  We detail the major challenges for Na-metal systems that at this time limit the feasibility of SMBs.  The core Na anode problems are the following interrelated degrdn. mechanisms: An unstable solid electrolyte interphase with most org. electrolytes, "mossy" and "lath-like" metal dendrite growth for liq. systems, poor Coulombic efficiency, and gas evolution.  Even solid-state Na batteries are not immune, with metal dendrites being reported.  The solns. may be subdivided into the following interrelated taxonomy: Improved electrolytes and electrolyte additives tailored for Na-metal anodes, interfacial engineering between the metal and the liq. or solid electrolyte, electrode architectures that both reduce the c.d. during plating-stripping and serve as effective hosts that shield the Na metal from excessive reactions, and alloy design to tune the bulk properties of the metal per se.  For instance, stable plating-stripping of Na is extremely difficult with conventional carbonate solvents but has been reported with ethers and glymes.  Solid-state electrolytes (SSEs) such as beta-alumina solid electrolyte (BASE), sodium superionic conductor (NASICON), and sodium thiophosphate (75Na2S·25P2S5) present highly exciting opportunities for SMBs that avoid the dangers of flammable liqs.  Even SSEs are not immune to dendrites, however, which grow through the defects in the bulk pellet, but may be controlled through interfacial energy modification.  We conclude with a discussion of the key research areas that we feel are the most fruitful for further pursuit.  In our opinion, greatly improved understanding and control of the SEI structure is the key to cycling stability.  A holistic approach involving complementary post-mortem, in situ, and operando analyses to elucidate full battery cell level structure-performance relations is advocated.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqQ1buYt3eMx7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgLBFA4U5D_Ng"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXmsFCrtL4%253D&amp;md5=50399f6bff0463f55ea84f50d36fb802</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1021/acs.iecr.9b02029"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Matios, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Enabling safe sodium metal batteries by solid electrolyte interphase engineering: a review</span>. <i>Ind.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enabling Safe Sodium Metal Batteries by Solid Electrolyte Interphase Engineering: A Review</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Matios, Edward; Wang, Huan; Wang, Chuanlong; Li, Weiyang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Industrial &amp; Engineering Chemistry Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">58</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">With the merits of a naturally abundant sodium (Na) resource with similar electrochem. characteristics to that of lithium-ion batteries, Na-based batteries have been widely studied as the next-generation economical and practical energy storage choices.  Particularly, the Na metal anode possesses a high theor. specific capacity of 1166 mAh/g and low electrochem. potential of -2.71 V (vs std. hydrogen electrode), and it is therefore considered as the ultimate anode material for Na-based batteries.  Nevertheless, the commercialization of the Na metal anode is still largely hindered by several long-lasting challenges, namely, metallic Na dendrite growth and unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation.  In this review, we first go over the fundamental mechanisms assocd. with these challenges.  Then, we provide an in-depth discussion on the recent key advancements from the perspectives of liq. electrolyte optimization, artificial SEI fabrication, and solid-state electrolyte implementation.  Lastly, we highlight the promising aspects from each strategy for the future development of Na-based batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqbpcseh1JuXbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhGIWlngTuxpw"> &gt;&gt;                 10.1002/adfm.202300135</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D33%26publication_year%3D2023%26pages%3D2300135%26journal%3DAdv.%2BFunct.%2BMater.%26author%3DM.%2BMoorthy%26author%3DB.%2BMoorthy%26author%3DB.%2BK.%2BGanesan%26author%3DA.%2BSaha%26author%3DS.%2BYu%26author%3DD.-H.%2BKim%26author%3DS.%2BHong%26author%3DS.%2BPark%26author%3DK.%2BKang%26author%3DR.%2BThangavel%26title%3DA%2BSeries%2Bof%2BHybrid%2BMultifunctional%2BInterfaces%2Bas%2BArtificial%2BSEI%2BLayer%2Bfor%2BRealizing%2BDendrite%2BFree%252C%2Band%2BLong-Life%2BSodium%2BMetal%2BAnodes%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fadfm.202300135&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c09387&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fadfm.202300135&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Series of Hybrid Multifunctional Interfaces as Artificial SEI layer for Realizing Dendrite Free, and Long-life Sodium Metal Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Moorthy, Megala; Moorthy, Brindha; Ganesan, Bala Krishnan; Saha, Aditi; Yu, Seungju; Kim, Do-Hoon; Hong, Seungbum; Park, Sangho; Kang, Kisuk; Thangavel, Ranjith; Lee, Yun-Sung</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2023</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">33</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">42</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2300135</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium metal (Na) anodes are considered the most promising anode for high-energy-d. sodium batteries because of their high capacity and low electrochem. potential.  However, Na metal anode undergoes uncontrolled Na dendrite growth, and unstable solid electrolyte interphase layer (SEI) formation during cycling, leading to poor coulombic efficiency, and shorter lifespan.  Herein, a series of Na-ion conductive alloy-type protective interface (Na-In, Na-Bi, Na-Zn, Na-Sn) is studied as an artificial SEI layer to address the issues.  The hybrid Na-ion conducting SEI components over the Na-alloy can facilitate uniform Na deposition by regulating Na-ion flux with low overpotential.  Furthermore, d. functional study reveals that the lower surface energy of protective alloys relative to bare Na is the key factor for facilitating facile ion diffusion across the interface.  Na metal with interface layer facilitates a highly reversible Na plating/stripping for ≈790 h, higher than pristine Na metal (100 h).  The hybrid self-regulating protective layers exhibit a high mech. flexibility to promote dendrite free Na plating even at high c.d. (5 mA cm-2), high capacity (10 mAh cm-2), and good performance with Na3V2(PO4)3 cathode.  The current study opens a new insight for designing dendrite Na metal anode for next generation energy storage devices.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo1Aim_Qf-CS7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhGIWlngTuxpw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3sXht1Oku7jF&amp;md5=d98eec093ff57230587abaaa6d7eb0d9</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1002/adma.202209511"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tang, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, C.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Multifunctional Interphase Layer Enabling Superior Sodium-Metal Batteries under Ambient Temperature and- 40°C</span>. <i>Adv.
